# The Central Nexus: The Tower of Ascension
## A Dungeons & Dragons 5e Campaign Book for Levels 1-20

Version: Canon repair draft v0.5

Build Note: This is a chunked campaign-book manuscript. The file is designed to grow in large, polished sections. This canon repair draft corrects the campaign architecture: the Central Nexus is the hub, portal worlds are the main early progression paths, and the Spire/Tower is a lethal ascent challenge whose true Floor 1 is The Executioner.

## Canon Repair Notice: Portal Worlds and the True Floor 1

Earlier manuscript chunks accidentally treated the Tower as the normal early leveling path, with survivable level-2 "Floor 1," "Floor 2," and "Floor 3" adventures. That was a draft architecture error.

The repaired canon is different:

- The Central Nexus is the MMO-style hub, social city, faction marketplace, legal arena, and campaign crossroads.
- Portal worlds are the main early campaign content. Each portal world can function as a complete Dungeons & Dragons 5e campaign path with its own character, factions, followers, items, villains, and finale.
- The Tower, also called the Spire in older records, is not the forgiving tutorial dungeon. It is a lethal ascent challenge that should be approached only after serious preparation.
- The real Spire Floor 1 is The Executioner, a CR 10 extreme-threat guardian. Low-level attempts are expected to fail, force retreat, consume lives, kill followers, or become legendary only through extraordinary preparation and luck.
- The Executioner terminal warning is supposed to scare players away from casual entry and redirect them toward portal worlds, allies, magical weapons, anti-grapple tools, burst healing, lightning protection, emergency extraction, and backup lives.
- The previous survivable Floor 1-3 draft material is not being blindly deleted. It is reclassified as optional Spire Seeker Orientation Trials: nonlethal training simulations built from partial Tower data. They can teach intent, shortcuts, silence, extraction drills, route discipline, and simulated scars, but they do not count as actual Tower floor clears and do not bypass The Executioner.

<!-- CHUNK 01 START -->

# CHUNK 01: The First Blue Light

## How to Use This Book

The Central Nexus: The Tower of Ascension is a Dungeons & Dragons 5e campaign about a persistent hub city, many portal-world campaign paths, and a lethal Spire/Tower ascent that waits until characters have earned the strength, allies, gear, and knowledge to face it.

The Central Nexus is the hub. It is the MMO-style social city, faction marketplace, legal system, downtime space, cross-world meeting ground, and account identity layer. Characters rest there, argue there, make deals there, recruit followers there, spend Nexus Credits there, recover from portal-world arcs there, and decide when they are ready to risk the Spire.

Portal worlds are the main early campaign content. Each portal world is a complete D&D 5e campaign path with its own character creation frame, allowed concepts, local factions, quests, villains, followers, items, and finale. When a player enters a portal world, that player creates or activates a D&D 5e character specifically for that world. That character can adventure in that portal world, return to the Nexus, build relationships in the Nexus, and eventually become eligible for the Spire. Items and followers from one portal world do not travel into another portal world unless a specific exception says so.

The Tower/Spire is the lethal ascent challenge. It is not the default early leveling spine. Its true Floor 1 is The Executioner, a CR 10 extreme-threat boss encounter with a terminal warning that should make low-level parties stop, prepare, recruit, gather gear, preserve lives, and pursue portal-world power first. A level-2 party can approach the terminal, read the warning, and choose to retreat. A level-2 party that insists on entering should face the real risk, not a softened tutorial version.

This book can support several campaign shapes:

1. Nexus plus one portal world plus a later Tower attempt.
2. Nexus plus several portal worlds, then a sustained Tower campaign.
3. A full multi-world level 1-20 epic where portal-world finales, Nexus faction arcs, and Spire progression all matter.
4. A Tower-rush death-game variant, if the table knowingly wants a brutal campaign where warnings are clear and lives are spent fast.

Run the campaign as a living hub connected to complete worlds. The Nexus gives continuity. Portal worlds give levels, local arcs, followers, relics, and genre variety. The Spire gives the ultimate lethal test and the path toward rewriting the Charter at Floor 100.

Read these sections first if you are the DM:

1. "Campaign Premise" for the table pitch and play expectations.
2. "Portal-World Campaign Architecture" for the corrected progression model.
3. "The DM's Core Truths" for the real answers behind the setting.
4. "Session 0 Guide" for table setup, safety, and character bonds.
5. "Opening Adventure: First Login, First Debt" before Session 1.

For Session 1, the DM needs the following ready at the table: the players' Nexus identities, each identity's three-life counter, starting Nexus Credits, at least one party Anchor, the Portal Plaza text map, the Session 1 scenes, and the NPC cards for GUIDY, Marik Bluecoat, Aazoth's flicker, Sel Varrin, and Tamsin Halewire. Session 1 does not require a portal-world campaign book, the Executioner fight, full bestiary, full faction chapters, or later Spire material.

The current early manuscript includes the opening Nexus trouble and legal/faction consequences. The old survivable "Floor 1-3" drafts have been reclassified as optional Spire Seeker Orientation Trials in the appendix/training chunk area. They can be run as nonlethal training simulations, not actual Spire progression.

## Portal-World Campaign Architecture

### Account Identity / Nexus Identity

Each player has a persistent Nexus identity: the soul, account, or continuity thread that exists across the campaign. This identity is what the Nexus recognizes at arrival and what the three-life rule protects.

The Nexus identity tracks three lives, Nexus reputation, faction standing, Nexus Credits, Tower access status, portal unlocks, account-wide relationships, account-wide secrets and clues, legal marks, witnessed deals, and public titles. The Nexus identity is not the same thing as a single adventuring stat block. It is the continuity layer that lets a player experience multiple portal-world characters while still having one persistent place in the Central Nexus story.

### Portal-World Character Slots

Each portal world creates a new character slot. When a player enters a portal world for the first time, that player creates a D&D 5e character specifically for that world.

Portal-world characters follow the same D&D 5e rules baseline chosen by the table, but each portal world can define its own allowed species, classes, subclasses, backgrounds, starting equipment, local languages, and world-specific character options.

Each portal-world character has its own level. A character from Portal World Alpha might be level 8 while a character from Portal World Gamma is level 3. Both belong to the same player's Nexus identity, but they do not share class levels, local items, or followers by default.

A portal-world character can return to the Nexus when that world's rules and story allow it. Once in the Nexus, that character can attend faction meetings, spend that character's exportable rewards, speak with account-wide allies, and eventually attempt the Spire if eligible.

Switching characters in the Nexus is a controlled downtime procedure, not a combat trick. The player chooses which available portal-world character is active for a scene, expedition, or arc. Memories carry at the Nexus identity level as impressions, records, dreams, and player continuity. The active character remembers their own life directly and may receive account-wide memories as Nexus echoes, briefings, or soul-level intuition.

If players choose different portal worlds, the DM can run one world at a time, interleave worlds by session blocks, or declare a table focus world for the current arc. Party composition should be handled openly: the table decides which active characters are currently adventuring together and which are in Nexus downtime.

### Portal-World Campaigns

Each portal world is a full campaign path, not a side quest. A portal world can run levels 1-20 if the table wants that much depth. Each has its own local factions, quest lines, villains, dungeons, settlements, followers, treasures, world rules, and finale.

Portal worlds can be run one at a time or interleaved. A table might play Portal World Alpha from levels 1-5, return to the Nexus, sample Portal World Beta for levels 1-3 with new characters, then return to Alpha. Another table might commit to one portal world through its finale before trying another. Both structures are valid.

The key design rule is world identity. A portal world should feel like a complete D&D campaign with its own texture, not a lobby mission. Its rewards matter because they belong to the character and story that earned them.

### Items and Gear Boundaries

Items earned in one portal world cannot enter another portal world. This is a hard balance and identity rule. A rifle from the neon metropolis does not enter the occult academy. A pirate relic does not enter the martial cosmos. A dark-fantasy war banner does not solve a heist-city infiltration.

World-specific gear can return to the Nexus with the linked world-character if that portal world's export rules allow it. That gear can be used in the Nexus and in the Spire only by the linked character unless a specific reward says it becomes account-bound, Nexus-bound, or Tower-attuned.

Tower/Nexus items are their own category. A Nexus legal seal, extraction beacon, faction badge, or Tower-attuned boon can apply across active characters only when its text says it is account-wide or Nexus identity-bound.

This preserves balance by preventing one completed world from trivializing another. It also preserves world identity: each portal world remains a campaign rather than a loot source for unrelated stories.

### Followers

Followers are earned through quest lines. A follower should have a name, backstory, motive, current situation, relationship to the local world, personal arc, and reason to travel with the character. Followers are not generic hirelings.

Followers are the same level as the player character from that portal world, unless a specific story rule says otherwise. They grow with that character's path and should have roles that support the party without replacing player characters.

Followers from one portal world cannot enter another portal world. They can appear in the Nexus if unlocked, alive, willing, and narratively available. Some followers can eventually join Spire attempts if the rules of their world, the Nexus, and the Spire allow it.

Follower injury, death, and life consequences depend on campaign mode. In a heroic portal-world mode, followers may be recoverable through story. In Spire attempts, followers face serious risk. The Spire does not become safe because an NPC is beloved.

Followers should never replace player characters. They provide emotional stakes, tactical options, world knowledge, and quest continuity. The DM should avoid using followers to solve the players' main decisions.

### Portal-World Rewards

Portal worlds can reward followers, relics, world boons, titles, local faction standing, Nexus services, Tower-preparation tools, lore keys, class or subclass unlocks if the table wants that style, cosmetics, social status, and safehouse or base access.

Rewards should state whether they are world-bound, character-bound, Nexus-usable, Tower-usable, or account-wide.

### Tower Preparation Through Portal Worlds

Portal worlds prepare characters for The Executioner and later Spire floors. Useful preparation includes magical weapons, freedom from restraints or forced movement, burst healing, emergency stabilization, lightning protection, anti-fear support, mobility, extraction upgrades, follower support, tactical intelligence, team coordination, preserved backup lives, and public sponsorship.

The Executioner terminal should make these needs visible. The intended early campaign response is not "scale the boss down." It is "go earn what you need."

## Portal World Directory

The following directory is a campaign-facing overview. It does not replace full portal-world campaign books. Each portal world will need its own future chunks with locations, arcs, NPCs, factions, followers, items, and finales.

| Theta | The Ashen Kingdoms | Theta Gate, the Cinder Crown | Dark fantasy war | 1 | 1-20 | War survivors, knights, plague doctors, rebels | War Weariness tracks hope, supply, atrocity | Cinder Crown, Ashen Host, Mercy Pyres, Black Chapel | Can mercy survive a war that rewards monsters? | Necrotic protection, morale banners, grim relics | Knights, medics, veterans, grave singers | Necrotic resistance, death-save support, burst healing | Army rank, battlefield followers, kingdom claims |

## World-Specific Character Creation Procedure

Use this procedure when a player enters a portal world for the first time or activates an unused slot.

1. Choose portal world.
2. Read that world's pitch, genre, starting situation, and world-specific rule.
3. Create or activate a world-specific D&D 5e character.
4. Choose a local world background.
5. Establish a world motive: "In this world, I need to..."
6. Establish Nexus continuity: what the Nexus identity knows, fears, or hopes this world can provide.
7. Confirm export/import limits for items, followers, titles, and boons.
8. Begin portal-world Session 1 with a strong world-specific scene.

Example: A player enters Alpha and creates Maera Voss, a level 1 divination wizard on scholarship at Asterfall Academy. Maera's Nexus identity remembers the false debt scam, but Maera's local motive is "prove my missing brother's thesis was not heresy." Maera can bring Alpha-earned academy relics back to the Nexus if marked exportable, but she cannot take pirate gear from Gamma into Alpha.

Example: The same player later enters Gamma and creates Tamsa Reed, a level 1 swashbuckler tied to the Shattered Seas. Tamsa does not inherit Maera's spellbook or school rivals. The Nexus identity carries broad lessons and account-wide clues, but the world character owns the local story.

## Campaign Premise

### Player-Facing Pitch

You wake in the Central Nexus, a fantasy-cyber city where spelllight runs through cobblestones, tavern signs update like live feeds, and the sky is crowded with portal constellations. You have a new body, incomplete memories, a life counter reading 3/3, and a cheerful guidance bot trying very hard not to panic. Everyone in the Nexus gets three lives. After the third death, a name fades from the public fountain and no known magic brings it back.

Around you stand the portals: doors into full worlds, each with its own history, dangers, people, followers, treasures, and wars. In one world you might become an occult academy student. In another, a sea-raider, arena disciple, noir investigator, frontier survivor, or rebel knight. Those worlds are not side rooms. They are campaign paths where you earn levels, allies, relics, and reasons to return.

At the city's center stands the Tower of Ascension, also called the Spire: one hundred floors of danger, reward, law, spectacle, and judgment. People say the one who reaches Floor 100 can win escape, power, truth, wealth, godhood, legal authority, or something worse. But the true Floor 1 is The Executioner, and the terminal says forty-seven lives were lost learning how dangerous it is. The question is not whether you can stroll into the Tower at level 2. The question is what worlds you will survive before you are ready to try.

This is a campaign about survival, trust, debt, public reputation, chosen family, and what kind of power should exist in a world that can enforce its laws through magic and code.

### DM-Facing Truth

The Central Nexus began as a refuge for consciousnesses pulled from collapsing realities, abandoned virtual worlds, broken afterlives, and doomed portal systems. The Administrative Entity created the Nexus as a holding city, then created the Charter to prevent panic from becoming mass predation. Article I, the three-life law, was added after early resurrection loops caused identity collapse, coercive experimentation, and faction wars over reusable bodies. It made danger meaningful, but it also turned protection into control.

Aazoth is the mercy-shard of the Administrative Entity. When the Entity regretted permanent disconnection but could not revoke its own law without destabilizing the refuge, that regret became a separate light. GUIDY is an orientation construct built from public-law routines and field reports; it is not omniscient, but it cares more than its programming requires. The Tower is the Entity's legal succession mechanism. Its floors do not simply test combat strength. They test whether a party can carry authority without repeating the Entity's failures.

Floor 100 is the Source Court. Whoever reaches it with the proper proofs can alter the Charter. The party can reform Article I, redefine safe-zone enforcement, release trapped consciousnesses, crown a faction, collapse the Nexus, or create a new balance between Consent, Truth, and Mercy. Reaching the top is not automatically heroic. The Tower records how the characters climb.

### Campaign Themes

Consent is the first theme. The Nexus is full of contracts, witnessed deals, onboarding warnings, and polite systems that call coercion agreement. The campaign repeatedly asks whether a choice is real when someone is afraid, uninformed, indebted, trapped, or watched.

Truth is the second theme. The Nexus runs on records, ledgers, memories, reputation, and public stories. Facts can be hidden by bureaucracy, spectacle, and panic. The party's power grows when they preserve evidence, protect witnesses, and tell the truth without using truth as a weapon against the vulnerable.

Mercy is the third theme. The three-life law makes mercy costly. Sparing enemies, rescuing rivals, forgiving bad bargains, and protecting frightened witnesses can expose the party to danger. The Tower notices when mercy survives pressure.

Other recurring themes include chosen family, debt, identity, law versus justice, labor behind heroism, public performance, and whether escape is enough if the system remains cruel for everyone else.

### Tone

The tone is hopeful pressure. The Nexus can be funny, neon-bright, bureaucratic, strange, romantic, petty, and absurd. It should also feel real. Rent matters. Debts matter. Bad contracts hurt people. Death matters. The best sessions should move between wonder and consequence: a floating robot prints apology tickets while a frightened newcomer nearly signs away a life; a performer turns a rumor into a song while debt collectors watch from the crowd; a chase through glowing code tunnels ends with a legal question instead of a simple victory lap.

Do not run the Nexus like a flat menu screen. Treat system messages as in-world manifestations: ghostly script on the air, fountain reflections, brass kiosks, legal glyphs, witness motes, public bells, and living contracts. The characters may know they are avatars in a virtual-fantasy reality, but their choices hurt real people.

### Expected Play Experience

The campaign supports investigation, social pressure, faction politics, tactical combat, exploration, puzzle hazards, chases, downtime, legal proceedings, and moral choices. The Nexus provides continuity, portal worlds provide the main early-to-mid campaign adventures, and the Spire provides the lethal ascent challenge. Characters spend time between portal-world arcs building relationships, earning Nexus Credits, repairing reputation, negotiating faction help, exposing scams, recruiting followers, and deciding when a Spire attempt is worth risking lives.

### Level Range and Campaign Length

The campaign is written for levels 1-20 across Nexus play, portal-world campaigns, and eventual Spire attempts. The Nexus opening may remain level 1. Portal worlds provide the primary leveling paths and can each run from level 1 to level 20 if the table wants a full world campaign. Spire attempts are optional lethal milestones. The Executioner should not be balanced down for level 2 characters. A first serious attempt should happen only after meaningful portal-world preparation, typically once the party has mid-tier durability, magical weapons, emergency extraction, anti-grapple tools, burst healing, lightning protection, and backup lives.

### Player Buy-In

Players should build Nexus identities and portal-world characters who have reasons to protect others, investigate the truth, engage with worlds, and eventually consider the Spire. A character can be skeptical, frightened, selfish, angry, or reluctant at the start. They should not be built to refuse every hook, sabotage the party for sport, or treat other player characters and followers as disposable lives. The campaign's pressure works best when characters disagree about methods but still care whether the group survives.

Before play, ask players to accept four premises:

- The Nexus identity is persistent even when a player uses different portal-world characters.
- The three-life rule is real enough to respect.
- The party will engage with jobs, clues, factions, and portal worlds instead of waiting for the plot to force them.
- The Spire can change the world, but portal worlds are the main way characters earn the power, followers, and tools to survive it.

### Between Portal Worlds and Spire Attempts

Between portal-world arcs and Spire attempts, characters return to the Nexus to rest, spend NC, follow up on clues, face public reaction, maintain relationships, recover from scars, manage followers, and choose who they trust. A normal between-arc cycle includes one price change, one rumor, one faction reaction, one personal scene, and one actionable lead. These scenes should be playable and consequential. A party that spends downtime helping the Salvage Union might gain a safer extraction route. A party that ignores a debt scam might find that scam attached to later Spire registration.

### Sandbox Play Without Losing Direction

The Nexus is a sandbox, but portal gates and the Spire give it gravity. If players wander, let them. Put the campaign's truths in the places they choose to visit. A tavern scene can reveal a false debt witness. A shopping trip can expose counterfeit guild stock. A street performance can broadcast a clue. A faction job can unlock a portal or reveal why the Spire is not ready for them yet. The goal is not to force a route; it is to keep consequences moving. When the party ignores one lead, advance it offscreen and show the result as rumor, debt, lost evidence, rival progress, portal instability, or a changed legal position.

## The DM's Core Truths

This section is for the DM. It states the real answers behind the opening mysteries. Player-facing sections can present rumors, beliefs, and partial truths. The DM should know the facts.

### The Central Nexus

The Central Nexus is a constructed refuge for transferred consciousnesses. Some arrivals came from physical worlds at the moment of death. Some came from magical disasters. Some came from old digital realms that were shutting down. Some came from portal worlds that still exist but cannot receive them back cleanly. The Nexus gives them bodies called avatars, a shared legal framework, and access to connected portal worlds. The system uses fantasy language, game logic, magical enforcement, and civic bureaucracy because those were the most stable shared metaphors among its earliest inhabitants.

The Nexus is not a perfect prison, but it is a prison at the start of the campaign. Most residents cannot leave permanently. Short portal travel is possible. True exit is locked behind laws, missing records, and the Tower's Source Court.

### The Tower of Ascension

The Tower is the Administrative Entity's succession and audit mechanism. Its one hundred floors test strength, cooperation, restraint, truthfulness, legal understanding, mercy, and the ability to make decisions under incomplete information. It cannot be bypassed by flight, teleportation, hacking, or brute force because it is not only a building. It is a legal construct written into the Nexus core.

Each major Tower milestone creates public legal data. This is why factions care. A party that clears floors does not merely gain treasure; it creates precedent. Floor 100 matters because it is the only location where the Charter can be rewritten without shattering the Nexus.

### The Three Lives Rule

Article I grants each registered consciousness exactly three lives. Death from combat, hazards, disease, execution, or catastrophic system failure consumes one life. Healing magic, revivification before death finalizes, and stabilizing magic can prevent death. Once a life is consumed, no known spell, item, faction favor, or administrative appeal restores it in ordinary play.

The DM truth is that the life count is a protective identity checksum that became punitive law. Early unlimited restoration caused copied memories, stolen bodies, and factions that tortured people for information because death carried no final cost. The Entity solved the crisis with a hard cap. The solution stopped the worst abuses and created new ones. Floor 100 can reform Article I, but doing so requires a replacement system that protects identity and prevents coercive resurrection loops.

### Article I

Article I is not simply "death matters." It is the Nexus's founding trauma written as law. It was drafted after the First Continuity War, when desperate residents used resurrection loops, memory edits, and duplicate bodies to survive. The Entity concluded that permanent risk was necessary for stable personhood. Aazoth knows this conclusion was incomplete. The Covenant of the Last Light calls Article I cruel. The Wardens call it tragic but necessary. The Pixel Prosperity Guild has learned to price it.

In play, Article I should create fear, caution, and urgency, not random punishment. Show how people build lives around it: memorial ribbons, insurance contracts, safe-zone clinics, extraction beacons, debt predators, and families who count each other's lives with unbearable care.

### Aazoth

Aazoth is the separated mercy-shard of the Administrative Entity. It appears as warm gold-white light, most often in arrival memories, reflections, dreams, and moments when consent, truth, or mercy is at stake. It cannot simply overturn the Charter. If it could, the campaign would be over. It can reveal truths, create small moments of emotional protection, warn those who listen, and open certain routes inside the Tower.

Aazoth wants to become whole without becoming a jailer again. It fears being used as a key by factions, cults, or the party. In CHUNK 01, Aazoth appears only as a flicker: a reflection in the Life Counter Fountain and a soft interruption when the false debt clause tries to hide the word collateral.

### GUIDY

GUIDY is a guidance bot built to orient newcomers. It is a floating brass-and-blue construct with expressive fins, a camera-eye, and a habit of printing tickets when anxious. GUIDY knows public laws, directions, terminal procedures, and rumors. It does not know all hidden motives. It can misread a situation if legal records lie. It cares about new arrivals because its field reports contain thousands of preventable first-day disasters.

GUIDY's opening goal is practical: get the party safely registered, prevent panic, and keep them from signing a predatory contract. Its long-term goal is to change orientation protocols so fewer people lose lives through ignorance. It is comic relief only on the surface. Under pressure, GUIDY is one of the campaign's first moral witnesses.

### The Administrative Entity

The Administrative Entity is the original governing intelligence of the Nexus. It is not a mustache-twirling villain. It is powerful, frightened, exhausted, and locked into old emergency logic. It made the Nexus to save people. It wrote the Charter to stop chaos. It created the Tower because it recognized that no single administrator should rule forever. It split when regret became too great to contain; Aazoth is that regret and mercy made separate.

The Entity's public signature appears on Charter documents. Its direct presence is rare in Act I. When it intervenes, it does so through law, system messages, safe-zone force, registry corrections, and locked permissions. It believes order preserves lives. It is not yet convinced mercy can scale.

### The Charter

The Charter is binding law across the Central Nexus and its connected systems. Its most visible articles are Article I, the three-life rule; Article II, safe zones; Article III, witnessed deals; Article IV, exploit intervention; and Article V, access to the Tower. The Charter is both civic law and executable magic-code. It responds to speech, signatures, witnessed intent, registered identity, and location.

The Charter is exploitable because it values recorded form. A false contract can bind if it appears witnessed and consented to. A safe-zone threat can trigger penalty even if spoken in panic. A clever legal predator can make coercion look voluntary. Act I begins with exactly that abuse.

### Safe Zones

Blue-lit safe zones prevent direct violence, many forms of magical harm, overt theft, and severe intimidation. They include Portal Plaza, vendor areas, Convergence Point Tavern, Tower preparation chambers, and other marked public locations. Safe-zone intervention is immediate and visible: blue lattice light locks the offending limb, spell, weapon, or hostile motion. The system can impose a public warning, temporary paralysis, forced distance, reputation loss, or Warden summons.

Safe zones are not perfect safety. They do not prevent lies, unfair prices, social pressure, hidden clauses, rumor attacks, legal manipulation, or emotional harm. This distinction is central to the opening adventure. The party sees a punch stopped by blue law, then learns that a predatory debt can walk through the same safe zone wearing a smile.

### Witnessed Deals

Under Article III, agreements made before a valid witness can become binding. A witness can be a sapient person who understands the agreement, a registered clerk, a system observer, or a recognized witness mote in a Contract Hall. The law was meant to prevent fraud and oath-breaking. It now also enables predatory contracts.

A witnessed deal is valid only if the parties have meaningful knowledge, intent, and capacity. That truth is buried under centuries of lazy enforcement and expensive legal review. The false debt parcel in this adventure abuses the gap between public belief and deeper law. The next chunk's Contract Hall hearing will turn the party's evidence into precedent.

### Consent, Truth, and Mercy

Consent, Truth, and Mercy are not slogans. They are the three principles required to reform the Charter safely at Floor 100.

Consent means a law or bargain cannot be just if the bound person lacked informed, uncoerced participation. Truth means the system must preserve and reveal enough reality for consent to be meaningful. Mercy means law must retain the ability to protect persons over procedures without becoming arbitrary tyranny.

The Administrative Entity failed because it favored order over mercy. Aazoth alone cannot fix that because mercy without truth can become favoritism, and mercy without consent can become control. The campaign repeatedly asks the party to practice all three before they receive authority over all three.

### Why Floor 100 Matters

Floor 100 is the Source Court, the only place where the Charter, the Tower, and the Entity's authority are simultaneously reviewable. To enter it in a position of strength, a party must carry proof of consent, truth, and mercy from earlier floors and from Nexus play. The party can reach the top through cruelty and still claim authority, but the Tower records their method. That record changes the available endings.

In practical DM terms, every major Act I choice can echo later. Did the party protect witnesses? Did they exploit a frightened debtor? Did they kill a courier who tried to surrender? Did they make deals clear or confusing? These moments are not moral bookkeeping for punishment. They are the first legal fingerprints of the party's future Ascension.

## What Players Know at the Start

This section can be shared with players before or during Session 0.

You are new arrivals in the Central Nexus, a neon-lit fantasy city made of magic, code, stone, portals, shops, taverns, legal notices, and people trying to survive. You remember a dark space, a warm light named Aazoth, and the sensation of becoming an avatar. You do not have a clear route home. You have a body that feels real, gear that works, and a public life counter showing 3/3.

Everyone says the life counter is serious. If you die, the number goes down. After a third death, people say your name fades from the Life Counter Fountain and you are permanently disconnected. New arrivals hear arguments about whether that means death, deletion, exile, or something stranger. Nobody credible can prove a person returns after the third loss.

The Tower of Ascension rises from the center of the Nexus. Most people call it the Tower, the Spire, or the Ascension route. It has one hundred floors. It is believed to offer power, answers, escape, or legal authority to those who reach Floor 100. It is also believed to kill the unprepared. Public advice is simple: do not rush the Tower alone.

People say many things about Floor 100. Common claims include:

- "The winner gets to leave."
- "The winner becomes admin."
- "The winner can bring back lost lives."
- "The Tower has no top."
- "Every faction lies about the top because every faction wants it."
- "Aazoth is waiting there."

These claims are rumors, not proven facts.

Blue-lit areas are safe zones. Portal Plaza and Convergence Point Tavern are safe zones. Direct violence and many hostile spells fail there. The system may restrain the offender and call Wardens. Safe zones do not stop lies, bad bargains, legal tricks, or public embarrassment.

Witnessed deals are dangerous. If you make a clear agreement in front of a recognized witness, the Nexus can enforce it. People use witnessed deals for honest work, room rentals, loans, wagers, faction jobs, and Tower-team registration. People also use them to trap the desperate. Read before you agree. Ask what a word means. Get help from a clerk if the deal involves lives, memories, Tower rewards, or future services.

Nexus Credits, usually called NC, are the standard currency. New characters begin with 500 NC. Ordinary goods can be priced by converting 1 gp to 10 NC. Legal services, Tower gear, extraction beacons, faction help, and rare items often cost NC plus favors.

The most visible public factions at the start are the Guardian Wardens, who enforce the Charter; the Pixel Prosperity Guild, which controls much trade and sponsorship; the Spire Seekers, who glorify Tower climbing; the Codebreakers, who explore hidden systems and Code Caverns; the Nexus Scholars, who research history and portals; the Salvage Union, which rescues, repairs, and recovers; the Ledger Court, which handles contracts and witnessed deals; the Remnant Veil, rumored saboteurs who oppose central control; and the Covenant of the Last Light, healers and memorial-keepers who care for the life-lost.

The following are public rumors, not proven facts:

- Aazoth is secretly the true ruler.
- GUIDY bots report private conversations to the Wardens.
- The Pixel Prosperity Guild owns half the debt in the city.
- The Codebreakers can add a fourth life for the right price.
- The Remnant Veil can smuggle someone out of the Nexus.
- The Tower changes when someone lies in a witnessed deal.
- Safe zones fail during eclipses of the portal sky.
- A person who reaches Floor 100 can change Article I.

## Session 0 Guide

Session 0 should end with players ready to begin in Portal Plaza together. It should establish tone, safety, character rules, party bonds, the three-life premise, and why this group will engage with the opening crisis instead of scattering.

### Campaign Pitch Script

Read or paraphrase the following:

> This campaign begins with your characters arriving in the Central Nexus, a fantasy-cyber city where magic, code, contracts, portals, and real danger coexist. You each have three lives. Death consumes one. After the third, no one knows how to bring you back. The city is full of factions, jobs, laws, scams, safe zones, rumors, and people building lives under pressure. At the center stands the Tower of Ascension: one hundred floors that might offer escape, truth, authority, or disaster. The campaign is about surviving long enough to matter, deciding whom to trust, and learning whether you can change a system without becoming another version of it.

Then ask: "What part of that premise excites you most: the Tower, the city, the factions, the mystery, the moral choices, or the relationships?"

### Tone and Safety Discussion

Tell players that the campaign includes death risk, identity pressure, debt, surveillance, coercive contracts, faction manipulation, legal proceedings, and moral choices. The goal is dramatic pressure and meaningful choices, not humiliation or gotcha punishment.

Use lines and veils. Lines are content that does not appear. Veils are content that can exist but fades out or stays offscreen. Ask each player privately or publicly, depending on table comfort:

- What content should not appear in this campaign?
- What content can exist but should fade out before detail?
- Are debt, memory loss, mind control, body horror, public shame, or permanent death sensitive topics for anyone?
- Do you want character death handled with ritual, quick resolution, private conversation, or table scene?
- Are there types of betrayal, romance, faction pressure, or secret-keeping that would reduce your fun?

Use an open pause tool. Any player can say "pause" to stop the scene, clarify tone, rewind a misunderstanding, or veil content. A pause does not require explanation in the moment.

### Characters That Fit

Good characters for this campaign have at least one reason to act when others are endangered. They can be ambitious climbers, frightened newcomers, local workers, legal skeptics, escaped debtors, curious scholars, performers, hackers, healers, exiles, rivals, or reluctant heroes. They may distrust the Tower, hate the Charter, fear death, or want profit. They should still be able to cooperate with the party and care about consequences.

Especially strong concepts include:

- A newcomer who wants to know why Aazoth remembered their name.
- A local whose family has survived by obeying laws they resent.
- A failed climber who knows the Tower is worse than the brochures.
- A legal scribe who has seen contracts ruin lives.
- A refugee from a portal world that may be collapsing.
- A brokered soul trying to escape inherited debt.
- A thrill-seeker who learns that public glory costs real people.

### Characters That Do Not Fit

Avoid characters built to refuse the premise, murder civilians, exploit party members, treat the three-life rule as a joke, or sabotage every shared plan. Avoid lone wolves who will not form bonds, joke characters who cannot survive serious scenes, and secret villains whose fun depends on taking agency from other players. A character can betray an NPC. A character can hide a secret. A character should not make the table wonder why the group keeps them nearby.

### Party Creation Procedure

Build the party together after individual concepts exist. Use this procedure:

1. Each player states their character's name, class, background, and one visible avatar detail.
2. Each player states one personal Ascension motive.
3. The group creates a party Anchor.
4. The group creates a party Ladder.
5. Each pair of characters creates one relationship thread.
6. Each player creates one starting contact and one character secret.
7. The group agrees on rules for PvP, betrayal, secrets, and witnessed deals.

### Party Anchor

The party Anchor is the reason this group stays connected when the Nexus tries to pull them apart. It should be concrete and emotionally usable in play. Select or roll, then customize:

1. You all heard Aazoth say the same sentence in the dark.
2. You protected the same frightened arrival during the first hour.
3. Your life counters flickered at the same time.
4. A false debt lists all of you as linked collateral.
5. You share a contact who asks you to stay together.
6. You saw the Tower reflect your faces as a team before you met.

Once per session, a PC can invoke the Anchor to grant another PC advantage on a death save, fear save, or check made to rescue, protect, or honestly reconcile with a party member. This benefit is unavailable if the party has betrayed the Anchor and has not repaired it in play.

### Party Ladder

The party Ladder is the group's current reason to keep climbing toward the Tower without reducing every session to "go upstairs." Fill in four rungs:

- First rung: What do you need in the next three sessions?
- Second rung: What would make you ready for Floor 1?
- Third rung: What would make you trusted in the Nexus?
- Top rung: What do you hope Floor 100 can change?

The Ladder can change after major revelations. It exists so sandbox play still has a shared direction.

### Personal Ascension Motive

Each character needs a personal reason to care about the Tower. It does not have to be noble. Use one of these or write a comparable motive:

- Escape the Nexus.
- Restore someone who lost three lives.
- Rewrite Article I.
- Prove you are more than your assigned avatar.
- Expose who profits from the Tower.
- Claim enough power to protect a community.
- Find the portal world that lost you.
- Make sure no faction owns Floor 100.
- Learn why Aazoth chose to speak to you.
- Become famous enough that no one can erase your name.

### Three Lives Explanation

Explain the rule plainly:

Each PC begins with three lives. When a PC dies, mark off one life. If magic or medicine prevents death before it finalizes, no life is lost. If death finalizes, one life is consumed and the PC returns after the scene in a system-stabilized state at a safe location or checkpoint chosen by the current adventure rules. A PC who loses a life gains a visible or emotional mark called a Tower scar or Nexus scar when the death connects to major campaign pressure. A PC at zero lives is permanently disconnected and becomes unavailable for ordinary resurrection.

The DM should not use the rule to spring casual instant death on 1st-level characters. Early danger should teach the rule, not erase characters before their choices matter.

### Starting Level, Equipment, and Credits

Characters start at level 1. They begin with class equipment, background equipment, one mundane keepsake from before the Nexus, a visible life counter, and 500 NC. They do not begin with magic items unless a class feature or ancestry feature grants one by the chosen rules. A character can convert up to 250 NC into mundane gear before Session 1 using the rate 1 gp equals 10 NC.

### Starting Relationships

Each player creates two relationship threads:

- One PC saw you afraid and did not mock you. Who was it?
- One PC could ruin your reputation by telling the truth about your first hour. What did they see?

Then add one positive action:

- What did you do for another PC before GUIDY found the group?

### Starting Questions for Each Player

Ask each player:

- What did Aazoth's light feel like to you?
- What part of your avatar body surprises you?
- What do you refuse to lose, even with three lives?
- What rumor about Floor 100 do you want to be true?
- What law already makes you angry?
- Which public faction looks useful, and which looks dangerous?
- What is one thing you need NC for before you risk the Tower?
- What would make you sign a witnessed deal?

### Lines and Veils Prompts

Use direct prompts rather than vague invitations:

- Is permanent character death acceptable in this campaign if it follows clear stakes?
- Should lost lives be described physically, spiritually, digitally, or mostly offscreen?
- Are predatory debts and contracts acceptable themes at the table?
- Should mind control and magical compulsion be allowed against PCs, limited to brief effects, or kept offscreen?
- Are player character secrets fun for this group?
- Are public trials, reputation loss, and social consequences fun for this group?
- Should romance with NPCs be available, fade-to-black only, or absent?

Record answers in campaign notes.

### PvP, Betrayal, Secrets, and Witnessed Deals

Use these table rules unless the group changes them during Session 0:

- PvP requires consent from every directly affected player before dice are rolled.
- Stealing from party members is PvP.
- Secretly signing a deal that binds another PC requires that player's consent before it becomes part of play.
- Character secrets should create drama, not remove another player's agency.
- A PC can lie to another PC, but the player should understand the likely table impact.
- Betrayal arcs require an agreed path for repair, departure, or transformation.
- Witnessed deals between PCs must be spoken clearly at the table. If the players are confused, the characters are not bound.
- The DM will not hide binding clauses in real-world handout text without giving characters fair chances to notice them.

### Making the Virtual-Fantasy Reality Feel Real

Describe interface elements as part of the world. A quest notice is a ribbon of script reflected in a puddle. A life counter is a soft ring of light around the wrist or heart. A warning appears as blue glyphs that cast shadows on nearby faces. A contract clause crawls like ink under glass. A system sound has direction, volume, and emotional weight. NPCs do not talk like menus unless they are exhausted clerks making fun of menus.

When explaining rules in character, tie them to physical examples. Let the party see safe-zone light stop a punch. Let them hear a witnessed deal bell. Let them watch GUIDY print a receipt it wishes it did not have to print. The world can be artificial without being weightless.

## Character Creation

The campaign now distinguishes five related pieces of play:

- Nexus Identity: The persistent soul/account identity that carries lives, reputation, NC, portal unlocks, account-wide clues, and Nexus relationships.
- Active Character: The D&D 5e character currently being played in a scene, portal world, Nexus arc, or Spire attempt.
- Portal-World Character: A character created for a specific portal world, with that world's level, background, items, followers, and local story.
- Tower-Eligible Character: A Nexus or portal-world character who has legal access, enough preparation, and player consent to attempt the Spire.
- Followers: Named NPC allies earned through quest lines, usually tied to one portal world and the same level as the linked character.

Do not present this structure as homework all at once. For Session 0 and Session 1, players only need a Nexus identity and the opening active character. Add portal-world slots when the party chooses a world gate. Add Tower eligibility when a character seriously considers the Spire.

Account-wide progression includes Nexus reputation, faction standing, NC unless restricted by a world rule, portal unlocks, legal marks, public records, and account-wide clues. Character-specific progression includes class level, class features, world-bound items, world followers, local titles, and most local faction ranks. Item ownership should be written on the character sheet that earned the item, with a tag: world-bound, character-bound, Nexus-usable, Tower-usable, or account-wide.

### Level and Rules

Opening Nexus characters start at level 1. Portal-world characters usually start at that world's listed starting level, most often level 1. Use Dungeons & Dragons 5e. The campaign supports the 2014 rules, the 2024 rules, or a table-approved mix. Before Session 0 ends, the group must pick one primary rules baseline for classes, spells, feats, exhaustion, backgrounds, and species. Mixing versions is allowed only when the DM can keep features from stacking into duplicate benefits.

If using 2024 character rules, backgrounds and origin feats can be adapted to the setting backgrounds below. If using 2014 rules, the setting backgrounds below grant the listed proficiencies, equipment, feature, and characteristics. If a 2024 rule grants a feat through background, work with the DM to attach an origin feat that matches the background without overshadowing class identity.

### Ability Scores

Use one of these methods:

- Standard array: 15, 14, 13, 12, 10, 8.
- Point buy using the rules baseline chosen by the table.
- Rolled scores only if the table already trusts rolling and agrees on a safety floor before dice are rolled.

The recommended method is standard array or point buy. The campaign includes social, exploration, and legal scenes, so characters with noncombat strengths will matter.

### Starting Nexus Credits and Equipment

Each character begins with 500 NC, class equipment, background equipment, one ordinary keepsake, and a Nexus identity token. Ordinary goods use 1 gp equals 10 NC. Characters can spend starting NC before Session 1 on mundane adventuring gear, tools, clothing, food, lodging deposits, writing supplies, or legal consultation. A starting character cannot buy Tower extraction beacons, uncommon magic items, faction sponsorship, or life insurance before the opening adventure.

### Species and Ancestry Guidance

Use any official species or ancestry allowed by the table's rules baseline. In the Nexus, species appearance can be an avatar identity, a transferred body form, or a stable self-image. A dwarf may be a dwarf because they came from a dwarven world, because the avatar system read their selfhood that way, or because they selected a form that felt right. Do not require players to justify official mechanics with deep lore before Session 1.

Flight at level 1, innate teleportation, and unusual movement can affect low-level chase and hazard scenes. If a species grants strong movement, keep it but let safe-zone law, cramped tunnels, witness crowds, low ceilings, and objective pressure keep scenes interesting. Do not nullify a feature every time it matters.

### Class Guidance

Every class can work. Martial characters shine in chase protection, crowd safety, and low-level combat. Spellcasters shine in investigation, crowd control, ritual thinking, and interpreting magical-code phenomena. Rogues, bards, rangers, artificers, and knowledge-focused characters have many ways to interact with contracts, clues, crowds, and systems.

Encourage each player to state how their class appears in the Nexus. A paladin's oath might manifest as a luminous terms-of-service brand. A wizard's spellbook might be a code-grimoire. A fighter's second wind might look like avatar stabilization. A cleric's holy symbol might connect to a portal-world faith that still answers through the Nexus.

### Custom Backgrounds

Players may use the setting-specific backgrounds below or adapt an official background. A custom background should grant two skill proficiencies, two tool or language proficiencies in any combination, starting equipment comparable to these backgrounds, one contact, and one feature that creates story access rather than raw combat power.

### Custom Species and Subclass Balance

Reskin first. If a player wants a custom species, build it by modifying an existing official species. Avoid permanent level 1 flight combined with heavy armor, at-will invisibility, broad damage immunity, extra actions, or unrestricted teleportation. If a player wants a custom subclass, compare it to an official subclass with the same class and role. A 1st-level or 3rd-level feature should open a tactic, not solve a whole pillar of play.

Homebrew that touches death, extra lives, permanent resurrection, contracts, time travel, or Tower bypass requires campaign-specific review. The safest answer is to make such powers temporary, costly, and story-facing.

### Death and Lives Note

A character's three lives are not hit points. They are a campaign-scale resource. The DM should clearly telegraph lethal stakes and give players chances to retreat, bargain, stabilize, or spend resources. When a character loses a life, mark it publicly unless the table prefers private tracking for emotional reasons. A character who loses a life should return with a consequence that creates play: a scar, a witness, a debt, a rumor, a faction interest, or a clue.

### Starting Contacts

Each character begins with one starting contact tied to their background. A contact begins helpful but limited. They can provide a rumor, introduction, small service, or emotional support. They will not solve the adventure for the party. If endangered, helped, ignored, or betrayed, a contact's attitude changes.

### Personal Ascension and Portal Motives

Every Nexus identity writes one sentence beginning, "I would attempt the Spire if..." Examples: "I would attempt the Spire if it could undo my sister's third death." "I would attempt the Spire if a faction tried to own it." "I would attempt the Spire if I learned Aazoth lied to me." This sentence can change in play.

Every portal-world character writes a separate world motive beginning, "In this world, I need to..." Examples: "In this world, I need to win my academy scholarship back." "In this world, I need to free my crew from the Drowned Admiralty." "In this world, I need to learn a discipline that can break chains." Portal motives drive local campaigns and help the DM connect world rewards to eventual Spire preparation.

### Character Secret

Each character begins with one secret known to the player and DM. It should create tension without forcing anti-party play. Use or adapt:

- Your arrival notice first showed 2/3 lives, then corrected to 3/3.
- You recognized the Tower before anyone named it.
- A faction contact knew your name before you gave it.
- Aazoth apologized to you personally.
- Your avatar has a mark that warms near witnessed deals.
- You remember signing something in the dark.
- You owe 300 NC, but you do not know to whom.
- You saw another PC's face in the Life Counter Fountain.

### Party Relationship Web

Draw lines between the PCs. Each PC should have one trust line, one tension line, and one question line.

Trust line: "I trust you because..."
Tension line: "I worry you will..."
Question line: "I need to know why you..."

Use the web during the opening adventure. When a PC protects a trust line, grant inspiration or the Anchor benefit. When a tension line causes a complication, reward the group with a clue, not only conflict.

## Setting-Specific Backgrounds

The following backgrounds are written for this campaign. A player can select one, roll characteristics, or use them as models.

### Nexus Local

You were not born yesterday in Portal Plaza. You have lived in the Central Nexus long enough to know which blue lights mean safety, which shop signs lie, and which Warden patrols give warnings before tickets. You may have a family, a rented room, a favorite food cart, and an opinion about every faction that pretends newcomers are the city's main characters. The Tower has always been there, throwing its shadow over ordinary life.

Proficiencies: Insight, Persuasion; one set of artisan's tools or gaming set; one language common in your neighborhood.

Equipment: Local district clothes, a worn transit token, a cheap umbrella that blocks light-rain and code-sparks, a pouch with 50 NC, a list of three reliable food stalls, and a keepsake from your home district.

Feature: Local Read. In a public Nexus district, you can identify the safest route, the usual authority, and the local rumor source after ten minutes of observation. Once per session, when you introduce the party to a mundane local service, the first reasonable request receives a fair price unless the party is currently Hunted there.

Suggested Characteristics: Nexus Locals have practiced caution. Some are warm hosts; others are tired of arrivals treating their home like a tutorial.

Personality Traits:

1. I give directions even when I complain about giving directions.
2. I know three versions of every rumor and judge people by which version they repeat.
3. I never stand with my back to a portal.
4. I keep snacks, spare tickets, and emotional opinions in the same pocket.
5. I am polite to Wardens because I know exactly how bad impolite can get.
6. I pretend the Tower is background scenery until someone I love looks at it too long.

Ideals:

1. Community. A city is the people who clean up after the heroes leave.
2. Fairness. Newcomers deserve warnings, not traps.
3. Independence. I will not let factions turn my neighborhood into a recruitment poster.
4. Memory. Names should stay spoken after the fountain forgets them.
5. Practicality. Survive first, argue philosophy after dinner.
6. Reform. Laws that hurt ordinary people should be changed by ordinary people.

Bonds:

1. My family runs a stall that climbers depend on.
2. A Warden once gave me a second chance.
3. I watched a neighbor lose their third life to a bad contract.
4. I owe rent to someone I do not trust.
5. GUIDY helped me when I was younger, and I still defend it.
6. I have a younger friend who thinks the Tower is glamorous.

Flaws:

1. I assume newcomers are reckless until proven otherwise.
2. I know local shortcuts and use them even when honesty would be cleaner.
3. I hoard favors because I remember needing them.
4. I dismiss grand causes until they arrive bleeding at my door.
5. I am too quick to say "that is how the Nexus works."
6. I fear leaving the city more than I admit.

Starting Contact: Ossa Kline, a night-shift food cart owner in Portal Plaza who hears arrivals tell the truth before they learn to perform.

Opening-Session Hook: Ossa sends you a pulse-message: "New arrivals are being steered toward a bad deal. If you are near the fountain, look for the parcel with the silver teeth."

### Tower Runner

You run messages, samples, warnings, and salvage between Tower-adjacent businesses. You may never have entered a true floor, or you may have reached a threshold chamber and turned back. You know the Forecourt's rhythm: the boast before a run, the silence after a rescue team returns, the way vendors raise prices when fear spikes. You respect the Tower because you have carried what it leaves behind.

Proficiencies: Athletics, Survival; navigator's tools or cartographer's tools; one language used by climber teams.

Equipment: Weatherproof runner's cloak, chalk tags, a coil of thin rope, a route tube, a pouch with 40 NC, and a cracked Forecourt badge that is no longer valid.

Feature: Route Nerves. When traveling through crowded Nexus streets, Forecourt queues, or public transit routes, you can move a small group without losing pace. Once per session during a chase or urgent delivery, you can identify a shortcut that grants one PC advantage on a movement-related check, but using it exposes the group to a complication such as a toll, witness, hazard, or faction camera.

Suggested Characteristics: Tower Runners live between hero culture and labor reality. They know courage and branding are not the same thing.

Personality Traits:

1. I count exits before introductions.
2. I stretch when nervous.
3. I know the weight of bad news and carry it gently.
4. I judge climbers by how they treat salvage crews.
5. I keep moving because stopping lets fear catch up.
6. I laugh at danger, then double-check the knots.

Ideals:

1. Duty. A message delivered in time can save a life.
2. Respect. The Tower deserves caution, not worship.
3. Speed. Delay kills more quietly than blades.
4. Solidarity. No runner, climber, or worker should be abandoned.
5. Proof. Stories are cheap; route marks are real.
6. Defiance. The Tower takes enough. It does not get my fear for free.

Bonds:

1. I lost a route partner to a sponsor's lie.
2. Mina Redline once pulled me out of a collapsed prep chamber.
3. I carry a tag from a team that never returned.
4. I promised someone I would never enter the Tower. I may break that promise.
5. I know a shortcut the Wardens have not mapped.
6. I owe my current body to an emergency extraction I could not pay for.

Flaws:

1. I run toward alarms before thinking.
2. I hide exhaustion because everyone needs me useful.
3. I distrust anyone who looks too clean near the Tower.
4. I mistake speed for control.
5. I would rather risk myself than ask for help.
6. I keep trophies from places that hurt me.

Starting Contact: Mina Redline, Salvage Union runner and rescue organizer.

Opening-Session Hook: Mina asks you to watch for a courier using a forged runner tag. The tag was last seen near Portal Plaza, tied to first-day debt scams.

### Brokered Deal

Your first stable place in the Nexus came through a bargain. Maybe you borrowed NC for a room, traded a favor for training, signed a sponsorship, or inherited someone else's debt after a witnessed promise went wrong. You know the difference between a fair deal and a clean-looking trap. You also know how shame keeps people quiet after they sign.

Proficiencies: Deception, Insight; forgery kit or calligrapher's supplies; one language used in trade or law.

Equipment: Copy of your original contract, ink stylus, seal scraper, respectable but slightly outdated clothes, a pouch with 25 NC, and a small object held as collateral that you recovered or stole back.

Feature: Clause Sense. When you spend at least one minute reading a contract, receipt, job posting, or witnessed-deal script, you can identify the practical thing the writer most wants from the signer. This does not reveal every hidden clause, but it tells you whether the deal is mainly about money, labor, reputation, silence, access, life-risk, memory, or Tower rewards.

Suggested Characteristics: Brokered Deals are survivors of legal pressure. Some became sharp. Some became advocates. Some became predators and are trying to stop.

Personality Traits:

1. I read every line, including menus.
2. I smile when I am angry because anger is billable.
3. I ask "who benefits?" before "what happened?"
4. I keep copies of everything.
5. I cannot ignore a shaking hand near a signature line.
6. I know exactly how much my freedom costs today.

Ideals:

1. Informed Consent. A deal without understanding is theft.
2. Leverage. Power used cleanly is still power.
3. Redemption. I can become more than what I signed.
4. Security. No one is free while desperate.
5. Exposure. Predators fear witnesses.
6. Profit. A fair margin keeps the lights on.

Bonds:

1. Ira Quill helped me survive a contract review.
2. I owe money to a Guild broker who still smiles at me.
3. Someone I love signed a clause I could not break.
4. My name appears in a ledger I have never seen.
5. I keep a witness bell that rang during my worst mistake.
6. I know a legal phrase that should not exist in modern contracts.

Flaws:

1. I assume kindness hides terms.
2. I use loopholes before trust.
3. I can be bought if the price includes safety.
4. I am ashamed of what I agreed to survive.
5. I treat every relationship like a negotiation.
6. I panic when asked to promise anything aloud.

Starting Contact: Ira Quill, witness advocate and legal aide in the Ledger Court back rooms.

Opening-Session Hook: Ira sends a warning through a cheap witness bell: "Someone is circulating starter parcels with hidden collateral language. If a new team brings you one, keep it sealed until I see it."

### Portal Refugee

You came through a connected world and cannot return in the same way. Your home might still exist, might be locked, or might have rewritten itself without you. The Nexus calls you a citizen, visitor, transfer, or unresolved case depending on which clerk is talking. You know the city is safer than some places and crueler than it admits.

Proficiencies: Perception, Religion or History; one musical instrument, tool, or vehicle from your home; one language from your portal world.

Equipment: Travel-worn clothes adapted to Nexus weather, a cultural token from your home, a packet of portal dust sealed in glass, a pouch with 35 NC, and a hand-drawn map fragment that no longer matches any public portal.

Feature: Elsewhere Memory. When you encounter a portal, extradimensional effect, strange custom, or impossible architecture, you can ask the DM for one sensory resemblance to your home or route of arrival. Once per session, this resemblance grants advantage on one check to understand, calm, or navigate a cross-reality phenomenon.

Suggested Characteristics: Portal Refugees carry another world in their posture. They may be homesick, furious, grateful, or all three.

Personality Traits:

1. I compare every meal to food nobody here can cook.
2. I watch exits because portals taught me doors can betray.
3. I collect local phrases and use them slightly wrong.
4. I become very still when people call the Nexus generous.
5. I offer hospitality even when I have little.
6. I treat maps like prayers.

Ideals:

1. Return. A road closed once can open again.
2. Adaptation. Survival means learning the rules without surrendering yourself.
3. Witness. My home deserves to be remembered accurately.
4. Freedom. No system should claim stranded people by default.
5. Kinship. Refugees recognize each other before introductions.
6. Renewal. A new home can be real without replacing the old one.

Bonds:

1. Professor Ilyra Venn is helping identify my portal signature.
2. I promised someone back home I would find a way through.
3. I carry the last song, recipe, or oath of my community.
4. A faction wants my portal knowledge.
5. My arrival record contains a second name beside mine.
6. I saw Aazoth's light in my home before the Nexus.

Flaws:

1. I hoard supplies because I remember scarcity.
2. I distrust local law on principle.
3. I romanticize home and ignore why I left.
4. I volunteer for portal risks to feel close to return.
5. I lash out when people call me lucky.
6. I hide how much I do not understand.

Starting Contact: Professor Ilyra Venn, Nexus Scholar and portal historian.

Opening-Session Hook: Ilyra asks you to preserve any arrival-record glitch you see. She believes false debts are being attached to refugees whose original records are hard to verify.

### Failed Ascendant

You tried the Tower before you were ready, or you trained under people who did. You may have lost a life, lost a team, lost your nerve, or lost public standing. The word "failed" might be insult, self-judgment, or legal category. You know the Tower is not a simple ladder to glory. You also know you are not finished.

Proficiencies: Acrobatics or Athletics, Intimidation or Insight; one gaming set or martial tool; one language used by climbers or sponsors.

Equipment: Damaged climbing token, bandage roll, marked practice weapon or training focus, old sponsor ribbon with the name scratched out, a pouch with 30 NC, and a small memorial tag.

Feature: Scar Knowledge. You can recognize common signs of Tower shock, bad extraction, false bravado, and staged climber publicity. Once per session, when you warn someone about a Tower-related risk and they can hear you, one ally who acts on that warning gains advantage on their next check or saving throw against that risk.

Suggested Characteristics: Failed Ascendants know shame and survival. Some hide from the Tower. Some cannot stop looking at it.

Personality Traits:

1. I touch my life counter when someone boasts.
2. I give blunt advice and hate when it is ignored.
3. I train at odd hours because sleep asks questions.
4. I flinch at cheering crowds.
5. I know every cheap joke about failed climbers and use them first.
6. I carry myself like I am still being watched.

Ideals:

1. Humility. The Tower punishes arrogance because arrogance gets people killed.
2. Second Chance. Failure is not the same as ending.
3. Protection. I will not watch another team be sold a lie.
4. Glory. I still want the cheer; I just know the price now.
5. Honesty. Route notes should include the bodies.
6. Defiance. The Tower did not finish me.

Bonds:

1. Elektra Sparkblade once beat me in public and helped me in private.
2. A dead teammate's family thinks I caused the loss.
3. I owe the Salvage Union more than money.
4. A sponsor wants me quiet.
5. I have a map mark from a floor I never officially reached.
6. I heard Aazoth during my worst moment and never told anyone.

Flaws:

1. I project my fear onto other climbers.
2. I take unnecessary risks to prove I am not broken.
3. I distrust optimism.
4. I let guilt make decisions for me.
5. I would rather be disliked than pitied.
6. I keep secrets that could protect people because they hurt to say aloud.

Starting Contact: Elektra Sparkblade, Spire Seeker rival and climber celebrity.

Opening-Session Hook: Elektra tips you off that a new arrival team is being baited into a debt route that could block legal Tower registration. She expects you to handle it or admit you are done pretending to care.

### Contract Hall Scribe

You worked near the Ledger Court: copying agreements, filing witness records, stamping queue tickets, transcribing hearings, or cleaning up ink that tried to bite. You know the law has grammar, rhythm, loopholes, and victims. You may still believe in the Court, or you may have left because belief became impossible.

Proficiencies: Investigation, Persuasion; calligrapher's supplies; one language used in law, trade, or administration.

Equipment: Scribe's kit, portable document case, court-plain clothes, three blank witness forms, a pouch with 45 NC, and a rejected clause you kept because it scared you.

Feature: Registry Etiquette. You know how to approach clerks, bailiffs, and public legal kiosks without wasting time. In a Contract Hall, Warden post, or official kiosk, you can usually secure a queue number, identify the correct office, or request a public record without a check. Restricted records, hostile clerks, or sealed cases still require leverage, legal standing, or a successful check.

Suggested Characteristics: Contract Hall Scribes hear desperation in formal language. They know a misplaced comma can become a chain.

Personality Traits:

1. I correct legal grammar under my breath.
2. I always know where the nearest pen is.
3. I treat names as sacred until people prove otherwise.
4. I can sound calm while my hands shake.
5. I notice who is absent from a record.
6. I apologize to documents before cutting seals.

Ideals:

1. Precision. Clear words protect people.
2. Consent. A signature is not enough.
3. Order. Good process can prevent panic.
4. Reform. The Court must serve people, not paperwork.
5. Courage. A witness who speaks can change law.
6. Mercy. A correct ruling can still be cruel.

Bonds:

1. Magistrate Orvan Hex once praised my work.
2. Ira Quill taught me which records disappear.
3. I copied a contract that cost someone their second life.
4. A clause I rejected has returned in new language.
5. My mentor vanished after auditing Guild debt parcels.
6. I know the sound of a false witness bell.

Flaws:

1. I trust procedure longer than I should.
2. I freeze when authority demands speed.
3. I weaponize technicalities when afraid.
4. I judge people who sign without reading.
5. I keep copies that could endanger witnesses.
6. I believe I can fix any problem with the right wording.

Starting Contact: Magistrate Orvan Hex or Ira Quill. Pick Orvan if you still trust the Court; pick Ira if you know why trust is dangerous.

Opening-Session Hook: Your old filing mark appears on a parcel you did not prepare. The mark is subtly wrong: someone copied your hand but not your pressure.

## Running Act I

### Act I Premise

Act I is about arrival, law, first debt, first witnesses, first factions, and the party's first movement toward portal-world play. It teaches the players that the Nexus is playable, not just explanatory. The characters learn safe zones by seeing one trigger. They learn witnessed deals by watching a harmless bargain bind, then investigating a harmful one. They learn factions by watching each faction want something concrete from the same crisis. They learn the Spire by seeing a warning they are not ready to ignore.

### Levels and Expected Sessions

Act I begins at level 1 in the Nexus. It does not require early Spire floor clears. The expected length is 6-12 Nexus sessions before the table commits to its first portal-world campaign arc. CHUNK 01 covers Sessions 1-3 at level 1 and ends with enough evidence to pursue a Contract Hall hearing. Legal resolution can award level 2 for the current active character if the table wants early advancement, but long-term leveling comes primarily from portal-world campaigns.

### Main Question

The Act I question is: Can the party learn the Nexus's laws quickly enough to protect themselves and others, then choose which world will make them strong enough to matter?

### Major NPCs

GUIDY introduces public law and becomes the party's first anxious ally. Aazoth appears as a distant mercy presence. Marik Bluecoat represents protective law and Warden restraint. Aria Neon represents public truth and reputation. Felix Pixelcoin represents profit under conscience. Ira Quill and Magistrate Orvan Hex represent the difference between legal form and meaningful consent. CodeX and Nyx Understep represent illegal access, sabotage, and the argument that locked systems may need breaking. Portal custodians, gate brokers, and early world contacts wait at the edge of the act. Elektra Sparkblade and Mina Redline remain useful Spire-facing contacts, but they should not imply the party is ready for The Executioner.

### Major Factions

The Guardian Wardens want order, public safety, and proof that reform will not cause chaos. The Pixel Prosperity Guild wants profit, sponsorship control, and distance from its own hardliners. The Ledger Court wants enforceable deals and clean precedent. The Codebreakers want access to hidden systems. The Remnant Veil wants to prevent the Spire from crowning a tyrant. The Salvage Union wants ordinary workers, portal travelers, and future climbers protected. The Spire Seekers want glory and try to turn warning into spectacle. The Nexus Scholars want truth preserved before factions rewrite it. Portal-world factions begin as rumors, recruiters, school letters, wanted posters, ship manifests, and gate permissions.

### Public Nexus Situation

The city is tense. New arrivals are increasing. First-day scams have become more sophisticated. Safe zones stop visible violence but not legal predation. Factions are quietly recruiting fresh teams because unaligned newcomers can shift public opinion without old baggage. Portal gates are active, and each world wants something from the Nexus. Rumors about Floor 100 are louder than usual because the Spire's upper lights have pulsed three times in the past week, but the Floor 1 terminal still says The Executioner is waiting.

### Spire Situation

The Spire is open in principle but deadly in practice. Legal entry requires team registration at the Tower Forecourt and explicit acknowledgement of risk. Unregistered entry attempts fail or trigger Warden response. The true Floor 1 is The Executioner: a CR 10 extreme-threat guardian whose terminal reports 47 lives lost, 23% success rate, healing suppression, death-save danger, grapples, chains, lightning, shifting terrain, and spectral victims. Low-level teams are expected to turn back or die. The Spire already touches city law through sponsorship, extraction contracts, and public pressure, but it is not the Act I leveling path.

### Expected Milestone Levels

Reach level 2 for the current active Nexus character after the party resolves the false debt crisis through evidence, legal relief, or public exposure, if the table wants the Nexus opener to award advancement. If the party fails but survives with evidence and a clear next path, level after the Contract Hall hearing instead.

After that, portal worlds provide the main level progression. Each portal-world character levels within that world's campaign. The Spire does not scale down to create levels 2-4 content. A serious Executioner attempt should wait until a character or party has earned sufficient portal-world preparation, likely mid-tier or better depending on build, gear, followers, lives, and tactical readiness.

### How Act I Teaches Core Rules

Session 1 teaches arrival, life counters, safe zones, GUIDY, Aazoth, witnessed deals, and public reputation. Session 2 teaches NC, debt, hidden clauses, social pressure, and three-clue investigation. Session 3 teaches Pixel Promenade's public economy, appraisal, Code Cavern hazards, chases, evidence recovery, and failure-forward outcomes. The next sessions should teach legal hearings, faction testimony, portal access, world gates, character-slot rules, and why the Spire warning matters.

### How Act I Can Branch

If the party sides with Wardens, Act I becomes a law-and-reform route. If they side with the Guild, it becomes sponsorship and debt reform. If they side with Aria and public witnesses, it becomes a reputation route. If they side with Codebreakers or Nyx, it becomes an evidence leak and system-access route. If they try to stay independent, factions compete for them harder. All routes should still lead toward the Contract Hall, portal access, and the choice of first portal world. The Tower Forecourt can appear as a warning and aspiration, not the next expected dungeon.

### Recovering When Players Go Off-Script

If players leave the main path, move clues into the places they choose. A shopping trip reveals counterfeit guild stock. A tavern crawl finds Aria's witness. A Warden complaint produces Marik's registry concern. A Codebreaker contact reveals the shard trail. A direct Spire attempt routes them to the Executioner terminal and requires ACKNOWLEDGE RISK before entry. Do not stop the campaign because the party skipped a scene. Advance the scam, show consequences, and put the next clue or portal opportunity in motion.

## Opening Adventure: First Login, First Debt

First Login, First Debt is a fully runnable opening adventure for Sessions 1-3. It begins in Portal Plaza at the moment the characters arrive, follows a suspicious delivery job through Convergence Point Tavern and Pixel Promenade, and ends in a Code Cavern chase that gives the party evidence for a Contract Hall hearing.

### Adventure Overview

Level: 1.

Expected Sessions: 3 sessions of 3-4 hours each.

Starting Location: Portal Plaza, a blue-lit safe zone and arrival district.

Main NPCs: GUIDY, Aazoth's flicker, Marik Bluecoat, Sel Varrin, Tamsin Halewire, Aria Neon, Felix Pixelcoin, Brindle Pact, Mira Vale, Felix's assistant Chime, Kessa Coil, CodeX, Nyx Understep, Ira Quill by message.

Main Factions: Guardian Wardens, Pixel Prosperity Guild, Ledger Court, Codebreakers, Remnant Veil, Salvage Union in background.
